I thought the story was contrived to make a bold statement against diamond mining, diamond smuggling and the diamond industry, in general. Well, I've never been a friend of the highly controlled diamond industry so it was good to see them take a little heat. They earned it. But to me, the total effect of the movie was negative. I ended up feeling as if I had watched a very well done and sophisticated piece of propaganda. When I see a good movie, I sometimes mull over it for the next few days. With a passion for traveling and understanding the simple yet earnest lessons in life, he protects his sheep just as they protect him.  Life seems to be pretty grand for the young lad, yet one night he has a dream that changes his life forever.  Nonetheless, one day, he travels to a small town and unexpectedly meets a heroin figure.  From their conversation, both Santiago and readers of the story learn of several conceptual life-meanings, some being the `soul of the world' and one's `personal legend.'  Perhaps this is the turning point of the story because everything after their meeting deals with fulfilling Santiago's personal legend--finding treasure.<br /><br /> Interestingly, the story is divided into two parts.  There are many distinctions between the two, with the first being the plot.  In part one, readers learn and grow fond of Santiago as bits and pieces of his life are extracted from the text.  In addition, he lives the simple life and showcases an ideal world in which fear is mostly non-existent.  Yet in part two, the name Santiago is never mentioned. He is then referred to as \\"the boy,\\" (81).  This may be to apply the story to not just the young boy, but to any other person.  This quality of the story admits careful planning and depth.  Moreover, the second section of the story deals with Santiago's dream of fulfilling his personal legend.  It could be stated that part one of the book lays the foundation for the rest of the story.<br /><br /> Secondly, the story plays with the concept of love.  In the modern world of today, love is sensationalized as something required to be happy.Yet in the past, it was sometimes quite the opposite.  Nonetheless, a common theme regarding love is the fact that at times, it can be immobilizing. At points in the story, Santiago is faced with the dilemma of following his personal legend or finding love, following motives or one's heart. This is especially true when on his journey, he finds a young woman whom he asks to marry.  Seeing this as a potential hazard to his life destiny, he is faced with either leaving her for treasure or staying, perhaps forever questioning his motives.  Coelho adds to the dilemma mentioning, \\"Wherever your heart is, that is where you'll find your treasure,\\" thus showing such decisions are quite hard to make (128).  This theme is interesting because it can relate to readers who have dealt with similar situations.  While love is not the only theme pronounced in the text, it is one of many to which readers across the world can relate.<br /><br /> Finally, the story ends without leaving any gaps.  While the climax of the story provides more action than nearly the whole story in itself, it is expectedly archetypal and heroic.  The climax may actually turn some readers off--perhaps because it is seemingly unrealistic, unlike the rest of the story--but it is sufficient in surprising the reader.  The rest of the story is fable-like, as Santiago finishes up his journey, but yet again, it is a bit unrealistic.  Even so, it ties in all points or themes revered in prior parts of the story, and leaves readers questioning their own personal motives.

B006ZCWU5K|5.0|Bruce Springsteen adds another great album to his list with the release of \\"Wrecking Ball\\".  It is primarily a \\"solo\\" effort as many of the E Street Band did not play on it.  However, unlike the other solo efforts this one is the \\"liveliest\\" sounding one.<br /><br />\\"We Take Care of Our Own\\" opens the album and sounds the most like the E Street Band to me.  Even though I like it and it is a rousing opener,  compared to what follows it is not the strongest song on the album.  But a \\"weak\\" song from Springsteen is one that many other would build an album around.<br /><br />\\"Easy Money\\", \\"Death to My Hometown\\", and \\"Shackled and Drawn\\" bring back pleasant memories of the Seeger Sessions days.<br /><br />\\"Jack of All Trades\\" and \\"This Depression\\" are both haunting songs and feature excellent solos by Tom Morello.  I will be curious to see how these will be played out on tour since Morello has a unique style.  However, I can also see Nils Lofgren and/or Springsteen taking on the role.  One need only hear the live versions of \\"Youngstown\\" to envision how Nils would approach it.<br /><br />\\"You've Got It\\" has been cited in other reviews as the weakest song on the album.  \\"Weakest\\" is a relative term when thinking about the Boss, but I enjoy slide guitar so I enjoyed this song a lot.<br /><br />\\"Rocky Ground\\" is the most un-Springsteen like song on the album.  It grew on me with repeated listening and the fact that female voices have a prominent role enhances it IMHO.<br /><br />Two \\"old\\" songs make an appearance.  \\"Wrecking Ball\\" made its debut on the last tour and was written about the tearing down of the old Giants Stadium.  Lyrically, the links is still there, but given the context of the album, it fits in just fine with its spirit of not looking back but looking forward.  The same goes for  \\"Land of Hope and Dreams\\".  I loved the live version of this from the \\"Live in NYC\\" album, but this version is superb, especially with its \\"People Get Ready\\" feel.  And it also serves as a last goodbye from Clarence as his solo lifts the song to the heavens.<br /><br />The song that is sticking with me the most so far is the closer \\"We Are Alive\\".  After the sadness in some the songs that came before, this tune ends thing on a hopeful note.  When I heard it the first time, I loved the haunting \\"camp fire\\" feel.  Then the \\"Ring of Fire\\" riff kicked in and I was in heaven.  I can't wait to see this one live as well.<br /><br />The two bonus tracks \\"Swallowed Up\\" and \\"American Land\\" are nice extras.  \\"Swallowed Up\\" definitely has the \\"Devils and Dust\\" vibe, which will be fine for some listeners and not so much for others.  \\"American Land\\" is rousing \\"closer\\".<br /><br />While his past solo efforts have been low key compared to his work with the full E Street band, this one rocks from beginning to end, often in ways that he not done so before.  I cannot wait to hear how they sound on tour with the power of the E Street Band.  Based on what I saw on the Jimmy Fallon show last week, Clarence (and Danny) are gone and will be missed but the band is rocking as well as ever and that is the best tribute!<br /><br />There have been critical comments about the validity of a rich man commenting on the plight of those less fortunate. Springsteen has always written about that and does it with insight and passion. The fact that he can still do it now is something to be lauded not regarded with cynicism.<br /><br />Those fans that prefer the \\"classic\\" sound and don't like their idol trying something new can enjoy his older stuff. But they are missing out if they dismiss what Springsteen is doing now. Unlike many other rockers his age who are still out there with nothing new to say and raking it in playing their old hits, Springsteen s always trying new ways to say things.  While he may not always succeed as much as fans would like, I hope he never changes!

1439102813|4.0|I devoured Still Alice in one sitting. Great read. The Harvard-educated Genova has a doctorate in neuroscience and weaves a thoroughly believable tale. It's genius, really: she writes from a third-person point of view (and I guess, realistically: the first-person POV of an Alzheimer's patient would require writing the same sentence over and over) but manages to focus almost entirely on Alice's perspective as the disease steals her mind.<br /><br />Having witnessed the progression of Alzheimer's disease, I'll say: Genova writes it spot on, and--for that reason, also her incorporation of scientific facts and research--I consider this book an educational tool. I will say that (in order to keep the ball rolling) she took Alice down fairly quickly, and those of us who have loved ones with the disease know: it doesn't always (if ever) work that way. The slow progression of the disease's stages (while, in many cases, the body continues to function well) can easily be its most challenging aspect.<br /><br />My only other criticism of the novel is purely personal: the most tragic thing about Alice's diagnosis--because of who Alice is, a professor of psychology at Harvard--is the loss of her abilities to work: not the devastation of her family. She and her husband retain, clearly, a high degree of admiration and respect for one another, but the stale quality of their marriage precedes Alice's diagnosis. Their three children are grown, and, for the most part, Genova doesn't show us their pain, although Alice's historically troubled relationship with the youngest child yields the two most touching scenes in the novel.

It's a little hard to believe that a 2-piece blues duo ended up doing songs for the NCAA championship.<br /><br />One really doesn't get the grasp of how insanely popular they would get on this debut release for Alive Records, 2002's The Big Come Up. Here, the junkyard band updates on the North Mississippi styles of R.L. Burnside and Junior Kimbrough, with enough modern twists on the genre to where they can call it their own. Half of these songs are covers or traditional songs, but with the right amount of dirt and rawness, they might as well be Black Keys originals.<br /><br />Highlights include the stomp of Do The Rump and Busted, the soulful cover of She Said She Said, and groovy songs like Countdown & I'll Be Your Man. Elsewhere, they put a dirty stomp on old traditional tunes like Leavin' Trunk.<br /><br />Obviously, Pat & Dan had a long way to go before selling out arenas. But if you want to start, start here.

B000F2C87Q|5.0|Written in between a transitional phase for the band, The Black Keys' last release for Fat Possum, 2006's Chulahoma EP finds the band at a great crossroads. Do we continue the dark atmospheric hill country blues of Big Come Up & Thickfreakness, or, do we bring in different influences and experiment a little? The answer is both.<br /><br />This is where the band truly shines: A 6-song tribute to Junior Kimbrough, father of hill country blues, this release has the timeless sound of an old juke joint in Holly Springs, Mississippi. Everything is there: The rough guitar tones, the endless drones, the thundering drums, the ancient production. But what also helps is the swampy psychedelic feel. Organ and maracas decorate Lord Have Mercy On Me, probably the most atmospheric song the band penned up to this point. It certainly led the way for richer albums like Brothers.<br /><br />All in all, this is probably the best thing The Black Keys have ever done. Later albums would abandon this flavor altogether for a more straightforward rock sound. It's a shame, because The Black Keys are one of the best blues bands out today.

B006ZCWU5K|5.0|This man is a gift... thank whatever god you pray to for him. Because there's a reason why people scream things similar to my review title about him. There's a reason why comments flood the internet trying to mock the fact that a millionaire actually cares about the working class. There's a reason certain people want Springsteen to \\"shut up and sing\\". It's because he scares them. Look at him... he's not supposed to be singing about police brutality and social justice!! He's supposed to be singing about how great America is!<br /><br />Anyways, on to the album. It's a fantastic disc. While Magic still stands as my favorite Springsteen album since Born in the USA, Wrecking Ball is pretty damn close. Miles above the atrocious Working on a Dream, which battles Lucky Town and Human Touch as the worst thing he's ever released.<br /><br />We Take Care of Our Own is both a rallying cry to help the poor and downtrodden, and also a stern criticism directed to those of us who have forgotten the whole \\"we the people\\" thing. A stunning song that really exposes a dark side of the American story.<br /><br />Shackled And Drawn, Easy Money, and Death to My Hometown are great rockers that should be blasted at every polling place this November. They get ya pump up, get ya angry, and get ya yelling. If I didn't know better, I'd say Springsteen was trying to lead a revolution! We shall overcome, indeed...<br /><br />Jack of All Trade has already had a reputation put upon it as a highlight of the album. With lyrics that would fit absolutely perfectly on The Ghost of Tom Joad, it's the kind of story that Springsteen does best. A fantastic case study of the struggle to remain hopeful in the harsh realities of America.<br /><br />The album doesn't hold up quite as well in the middle. This Depression is the first (minor) dud on the album. Its melody is uninteresting, while its lyrics vary oddly from nihilistic pessimism to corny with him calling out for his baby's heart. I just don't think the connection works, and think it's a little weak. Although this may change upon repeated listens.<br /><br />Wrecking Ball is a strange one. I saw him perform this song live, opening up the final show on the Working on a Dream Tour. I absolutely loved it. It became one of my favorite Bruce songs. But when I heard that not only would it be on the new album, it would actually be the title track, I had to scratch my head. Sure, it's a great little novelty song that marks a certain highlight in his recent concert history... but let's not forget he's singing about a friggen football stadium.  Whatever analogy he wants to use to justify it being on the album I just don't buy. It doesn't fit in. A great song, but just not here.<br /><br />We then come to the one horrible song on the album. Like, Queen of the Supermarket bad. You've Got It is so ridiculous in its unoriginality that one must wonder if Springsteen put it on as a joke. And add in the fact that this generic love song falls right in the center of the story of the death of America. What? Horrible filler. Its inclusion here is so baffling that it boarders on embarrassing.<br /><br />It doesn't last long though, because the next song, Rocky Ground, will probably go down as one of Springsteen's most appreciated song. It's sublime in every way. From incorporating the different samples to the gospel chorus to the rap section, it's perfect. This song alone shows the genius of this man. Here, at 62, he's still pushing the boundaries not only lyrically, but also musically.<br /><br />Land of Hopes and Dreams is obviously a great song that the fans adore, but its inclusion here bothers me. Not that it's not a fantastic song, but that it's more than 12 years old. He's been playing it since Clinton was in office. It's the second \\"not new\\" song to be included on the album, and while I do love it, I just can't seem to think that he's cheating a little including it. But it's a great song, nevertheless. A story of undying hope, even if this impossible \\"land\\" that he speaks of is of another world.<br /><br />We are Alive is a great closer. The music rises and falls while depicting several workers who died trying to achieve the American Dream. It offers hope in the face of the darkness. Fight for your rights, even if you'll lose. This cements the key idea that Springsteen has been trying to hammer home for 30 years. Community is essential in the fight for equality. We must unite against the harsh realities of a country under corporate control. Because it's not a fight against one group or one set of ideas, but a fight for the America we've all been told exists, but have never seen.<br /><br />The American Dream has been transformed into a nightmare, and Springsteen have been detailing it since its inception. From Nebraska, to Tom Joad, to Magic, Springsteen consistently stands on the correct side of history. He's a good Lefty that shows his dignity and integrity every step of the way. A necessary voice in a country that has swayed dangerously to the right-wing. He's an American treasure... a phenomenon that eases the pain of a fallen country. And Wrecking Ball sits conformably on the shelf along his other albums that consistently shine a light through the bleak realities of America.<br /><br />With Bruce on our side, how can we possibly lose? Bring it on!<br />-----------------------------------------------<br /><br />Let a man work!! Is that so wrong?<br /><br />(4.5 stars)

B005OK721G|4.0|The seriously annoying trailer (\\"Never forget where you came from.\\") almost kept me from watching this film. But I consider Zoe Saldana to be a must-see performer, and so I caved. And, as it turns out, COLOMBIANA is nowhere near as funky as that lame trailer made it out to be. Zoe Saldana brings out the sultry and submits a strong acting performance. If only the script hadn't undercut her. COLOMBIANA can't seem to make up its mind on whether it's more an action film or a dramatic one. No, we don't quite get a seamless blend of the two genres.<br /><br />Child actress Amandla Stenberg, with her stillness and poise and haunting silences, deserves all manner of props. She carries the first half hour of the movie, and you can't take your eyes off her. Stenberg plays Cataleya, a 9-year-old girl in gang-infested Bogota whose parents are shot up to bits in front of her.<br /><br />Fifteen years later, an FBI profiler (Lennie James, always good) is obsessed with nabbing an assassin who's committed 22 gaudy murders within the past four years, never mind that each of the assassin's targets is a \\"professional bad apple.\\" The FBI agent knows it's the same killer because he always marks his victims with an image of a Colombian orchid. Later he learns that the flower is called a \\"cataleya.\\" Connect now your own dots.<br /><br />If I hadn't seen Gina Carano in bonecrunching action in HAYWIRE, I'd be even more rhapsodic over Zoe Saldana's action hero creds. As it is, Saldana evolves from her badasss role in THE LOSERS. Thrust that much more in the spotlight, she easily carries the bits of the film that Amandla Stenberg doesn't carry. The similarity between the two actresses is remarkable. So credit Zoe for capturing Amandla's sense of gravity about herself, and also that quiet fierceness. For her part, Zoe combines her dramatic skills - her Cataleya looks so desolate at times - with a dynamic and, frankly, very sexy physicality. I like that the grown-up Cataleya - and, yes, she grew up to be a professional assassin - is calculating and meticulous. She plans her contract hits. She carries out her plans flawlessly. But the closer she gets to her ultimate target - him what ordered the hit on her parents - she allows her emotions to get to her. She becomes sloppy. It costs her.<br /><br />Michael Vartan, by the way, serves as Saldana's love interest. It's almost not worth mentioning this.<br /><br />COLOMBIANA is style over substance, despite Saldana's powerhouse presence. The problem lies with several over-the-top moments, those inane flourishes which ultimately undermine the strong acting. There are two particularly egregious scenes. Early on, 9-year-old Cataleya's gangster tio, to prove some sort of point, takes out his gun in broad daylight, in front of an elementary school, and shoots off a round which sends a passing car careening into a halt. As police sirens wail, the tio delivers his lecture - something about education as a building block for all assassins-in-training - and then they just casually stroll away, with apparently no one having seen the guy firing his weapon. This is an absurd scene. The other laying-it-on-thick sequence involves pet sharks in a baddie's pool. One glance at that, and you just know Cataleya will be incorporating the sharks in one of her kills. When the payoff arrives, it happens as a quick one and done camera shot. It's a death dealt too quickly, and, gorehound that I am, I felt cheated.<br /><br />Zoe Saldana in graceful action is why I tuned in. If she has a calling card as a mighty arsekicker, it's that one brutal hand-to-hand combat scene late in the movie in which Saldana transforms a towel and three toothbrushes into deadly weapons. It's almost as good a sequence as Cataleya's layered infiltration of a police precinct. Providing you can overlook the film's silly excesses, you may find yourself immersed in COLOMBIANA. Saldana is terrific.<br /><br />Just remember: \\"Never forget where you came from.\\"<br /><br />3.5 out of 5 stars.<br /><br />The DVD's bonus stuff:<br /><br />- \\"COLOMBIANA: The Making Of\\" featurette (00:25:07 minutes)<br />- \\"Cataleya's Journey\\" - includes interviews with Zoe Saldana and Amandla Stenberg (00:09:31 minutes)<br />3.5 out of 5 stars.<br /><br />The DVD's bonus stuff:<br /><br />- \\"COLOMBIANA: The Making Of\\" featurette (00:25:07 minutes)<br />- \\"Cataleya's Journey\\" - includes interviews with Zoe Saldana and Amandla Stenberg (00:09:31 minutes)

1451627289|5.0|I've been a Stephen King fan pretty much since he first started publishing books (yeah, shows how old I am). At one point, I stopped reading him because his writing started to get very generic. And then a few years back, he came out of retirement and started writing again. And let's just say that I've started becoming a King fan all over again.<br /><br />This book is definitely a departure from the usual King M.O. in that it's more of a science fiction novel than horror, yet throughout the novel there are King-ish moments that only a master of horror could possibly include. He takes us back to Derry (a town I will always know from the extremely creepy \\"It\\") but does it as by going back in time before all of the evil events took place. And then he casts a spell of including so many little references to his other works throughout the novel because his main character is literally living through time (shortly before the Kennedy assassination attempt).<br /><br />And then he brings us to a brilliantly researched tale through Lee Harvey Oswald's world as seen by someone who already knows what the man is going to do in a few short years. And then, because that would have been enough for any other great novelist, he creates a brilliant story for the protagonist who has to fit into the late 1950s, early 1960s in a small town in Texas where he build a relationship with a young woman who becomes a great focus for the story itself.<br /><br />Even to the very end, I still kept wondering what he was going to do to wrap it all up, and he does it amazingly. I'm not usually a 5 star reviewer but this is one of those rare books that I read all the way through and enjoyed almost every word. I know his next book brings us back to the Dark Tower series, but I'm probably going to take a break from King for awhile, happy that his last two books (this and Under the Dome) were well worth reading.

B0051ANPZQ|5.0|reposted from my blog: [...]<br /><br />A few weeks back, I ranted a bit (okay, a lot), about the absence of aspirational heroes in Middle-Grade fiction these days. For the most part, I'm still feeling tantram-y about the general state of things in this regard.<br /><br />HOWEVER--<br /><br />WONDER, by R.J. Palacio , goes a long way to correct this.<br /><br />Let's face it: there are books and then, there are BOOKS! And, by the latter category, I mean those rare gems that not only captivate us but also change us--for the better--by us having had the good fortune to have read them.<br /><br />WONDER is such a wonder. It tilted my obit, just a touch, and here's why:<br /><br />The story is about Auggie, a normal kid with a horrifying facial deformity. Yes, I said *deformity* although, like everyone else in the story, the impact of that word diminishes for the reader as we, too, get to know this remarkable little man.<br /><br />The story unfolds through the eyes of first, Auggie, then in sections devoted to the people who know and love him. It's a study in perspective---how each character views and copes with the daunting challenges of everyday life when you're always the center of unwanted, and sometimes hostile, attention.<br /><br />The writing is sublime perfection. Simple, and yet, so, so powerful. Palacio's voice carries the sophisticated eloquence needed to lift such a melancholy story into the realm of inspiration.<br /><br />One of my favorite passages:<br /><br />\\"Why do I have to be so ugly, Mommy?\\" I whispered.<br />\\"No, baby, you're not...\\"<br />\\"I know I am.\\"<br />She kissed me all over my face. She kissed my eyes that came down too far. She kissed my cheeks that looked punched in. She kissed my tortoise mouth. She said soft words that I know were meant to help me, but words can't change my face.\\"<br /><br />My heart aches, just typing that.<br /><br />There is so much wisdom in this book. I'm in no way surprised that so many have plucked pearls from its text. I am particularly fond of this one:<br /><br />\\"I think that there should be rule that everyone in the world should get a standing ovation at least once in their lives.\\"<br /><br />Wouldn't we all be better people if we gave more of those and received at least one?<br /><br />Read this book. You'll be grateful you did.d should get a standing ovation at least once in their lives.\\"    Wouldn't we all be better people if we gave more of those and received at least one?    Read this book. You'll be grateful you did.

0375869026|5.0|August (Auggie) Pullman was born with severe and life-threatening craniofacial abnormalities. After a young lifetime of wall-to-wall corrective surgeries, he still stops people dead in their tracks when they first encounter his visage.<br /><br />And now ten year-old Auggie Pullman, who has been kept relatively protected -- living in a New York City neighborhood where he is a known quantity, and being homeschooled by his mother -- is attending a real school for the first time. It is a private middle school to which his parents submitted an application without telling him first.<br /><br />WONDER took me by surprise. I've read enough stories about the outsider in middle school, so there are some things you come to expect. But what I did not expect was for the story to really take off a quarter of the way through when the point of view suddenly switches to that of Olivia, arguably the book's pivotal character. \\"Via\\" is Auggie's high school-aged sister, and she had once upon a time been a doted-upon first child and first grandchild until Auggie was born and immediately sucked up all the oxygen in the room with his medical issues.<br /><br />Then, further on, when I thought we would once again return to Auggie's perspective, we instead move on to the points of view of a series of other young characters who have become part of Auggie's story and have their own relationships with him and their own takes on and his situation.<br /><br />Sure, there are lots and lots of stories with multiple perspectives. But I am really impressed by the power brought to this story through the use of the format and through the respect that the author shows for her middle school audience in probing the honest and sometimes complex and sometimes unpretty emotions inside the story's young and adult characters.<br /><br />\\"her parents couldn't be nicer. put me at ease right away. the waiter brings over the menus and i notice his expression the moment he lays eyes on august. but i pretend not to notice. i guess we're all pretending not to notice things tonight. the waiter. my tics. the way august crushes tortilla chips on the table and spoons the crumbs into his mouth. i look at olivia and she smiles at me. she knows. she sees the waiter's face. she sees my tics. olivia is a girl who sees everything.\\"<br /><br />As I've previously pointed out, when I've written about other stories involving name-calling and intolerance, middle schoolers learning about acceptance amongst one another can oftentimes serve as THE pivotal step in their growing up with a predisposition toward accepting those individuals and groups from outside one's own community, religion, race, sexual orientation, and country. 1451683405|2.0|Sam Harris exploded onto the scene with the stunningly lucid and urgent \\"The End of Faith\\", which has become a modern classic and remains the very best of the stupidly-titled \\"New Atheist\\" genre.  While Sam's assorted essays and public appearances in the following years remained(and remain) a delight to read, my own personal enthusiasm for him plummeted after the shockingly bad exhibition of pedestrian and often times just plain nonsensical philosophy exhibited in The Moral Landscape, in which Harris apparently felt that dangling the talisman of neuroscience magically solved the quandaries that have plagued the pursuit of \\"objective\\" ethics for centuries.  The professional reviews were pulverizing and Harris' response to them thus far has been very inadequate. Would Free Will continue the foray into oblivion or signal the return of the author who composed the brilliant The End of Faith?<br /><br />To someone who hasn't given much thought to this issue previously, there is much provocative material to be had here.  Especially relieving is that Sam's neuroscientific findings/observations are indeed very relevant and illuminating within this particular debate, unlike in TML.  Indeed, upon finishing the opening chapters, it's very hard to conceive of a cogent argument for the existence of Free Will in light of the philosophical and,now, thanks to neuroscience, empirical arguments against the concept.  What's rather disappointing, however, is that Harris' acknowledges that his friend Daniel Dennett is a proponent of compatibalism, but is then very, very cursory in his treatment and dismissal of that school of thought; if you don't know anything about compatibalism before you read this book, you won't know much more about it after you finish this book.  Yes, I am aware of how similar this sounds to the ridiculous canard that New Atheists don't even engage with \\"authentic\\" or \\"sophisticated\\" theology, however I don't think that's an exact parallel.  Certainly atheists areright to point out that filling in gaps in our understanding of the universe with theology is untenable, however \\"sophisticated\\" it is.  But here Harris seems to be arguing that the \\"gap\\" in our human understanding of consciousness forever unbridgeable. He shrewdly points out that it's hard to even conceive in principle of what \\"free will\\" might entail, but even conceding that, it's not entirely convincing.  What's also noteworthy is that Dennett himself is a very able philosopher who often delights in excoriating vacuous philosophical arguments.  If even Dennett subscribes to compatibalism, surely it is on more substantive grounds than Harris presents in this book.<br /><br />The main problem I have with this work is the treatment of reconciling the lack of free will with an internally coherent way of punishing criminals.  Harris' argument is flawed on many levels.  First, his argument that stewards of people's neurological states could be held accountable for not \\"treating\\" the would-be criminal does not solve the fundamental problem, it merely shifts the moral culpability to another agent, who in turn ALSO HAS NO FREE WILL.  i.e, in Harris' argument, a doctor who withheld a cure for \\"evil\\"--a rather facile scenario--has committed a moral failing, so who is to be held responsible for that? This merely creates an infinite regress.  Even if we were to concede that such a form of punitive justice would be internally coherent, which it isn't, actually applying would raise some very real fascist implications.  Eliding this was a huge problem with The Moral Landscape and unfortunately rears its head again here.<br /><br />(There is a very interesting episode of the television program \\"House\\" that deals with the question of culpability.  In it, a convicted triple murderer on death row is found to have a \\"pheo\\"; a tumor that randomly releases shots of adrenaline which can cause fits of rage.  After discovering this, Dr. Foreman decides to testify at a hearing in favor reducing or ending the patient's prison sentence.  House agrees that Dr. Foreman can do this on his own time and that the argument has some merit, but also urges him to keep in mind \\"all those other pheo sufferers<br />who managed to control their rage attacks and become lawyers, race car drivers, and even doctors.\\" This episode is the Season 2 premiere entitled \\"Acceptance\\")<br /><br />Harris' dismissal of the illusion of free will being essential for happiness is far too cursory, as it is constituted entirely merely of Sam's personal feelings about not having free will.  Certainly there are others who share his viewpoint, but I personally become very depressed when I let myself get on a long train of reductionist thought.  Certainly others feel this way, too; this subject deserved more than simply Harris' own personal anecdote.<br /><br />Finally, while this work hasn't been published before in the traditional sense, for an avid reader of Harris' blog and assorted essays very little of the material is entirely new, but merely a stitching of earlier, shorter, materials.<br /><br />Overall, this is a mildly provocative book that certainly does a good job of deconstructing many of the illogical dogmas surrounding the idea of free will, but won't entirely convince a skeptical reader who is already aware of the traditional arguments about free will or the lack thereof.<br /><br />2.5 stars.

B000069HKH|5.0|When this album came out I was very shocked that Bruce Springteen was reuniting with the E-Street Band for the first time in over a decade and a half for a new album. It actually wasn't long before Bruce was back in the studio with the newly revived E-Street Band making a new album that did in fact seek to explore that issue. It would seem as if Springsteen might just be one of those people who finds himself working better with direct inspiration than anything else. As with most Bruce Springsteen recordings,he doesn't approach things in any lightweight sort of manner. It would've been all to easy for him to approach the topic of the post 2001 world with the emphasis on a couple of characters effected by it. But of course he didn't. Something more complex happened. As he realized around this time,the immediate \\"pop 9/11\\" world and it's \\"war on terror\\" was filled with so much racial/religious hatred and blind patriotism on both sides of everything that a well rounded treatment would be very much needed.<br /><br />  This is a slight bit different than a lot of the Bruce Springsteen albums that had come before. There were no direct commentaries on the singing fire fighters at the beginning of every TV show this side of SNL. Or of the media's self censorship of the era-delaying and often outright banning films and literature that might encourage any violence or (to a broader extenet) how American was handling the issues we were suddenly forced to contend. Those were important issues for sure. But at the same time by addressing the whole thing with his own unique style of commentary,as well as placing it in the context of a far broader musical base than he normally applied to a single album Springsteen not only re-awakened his recording career with a bang but had a whole new source of inspiration as well. He wouldn't have to find new ways to maintain his \\"working class hero\\" persona anymore. That persona had now matured into that of a seasoned and observant poet who was willing to bring all the musical styles he'd embraced over the years together,in one album,to tell this story. An era when a call to arms is no longer seen cynically as a marketing tactic and is again begin to have a realistic meaning to people. 1451683405|3.0|In his new essay, neuroscientist Sam Harris has declared free will to be illusory. He bases this monumental conclusion on the undeniable fact that in laboratory experiments neural activity occurs in human subjects' brains prior to conscious decisions. He just fails to consider the ambiguous implications of these experiments.<br /><br />He says: \\"You might spend an hour thinking and acting freely in the lab, only to discover that the scientists scanning your brain have been able to produce a complete record of what you would think and do some moments in advance of each event.\\"<br /><br />Thus encapsulated is the book's argument--as well as the fallacy of that argument. Yes, neural activity takes place prior to decision-making, but to state that what a subject might be thinking is in any way available to someone privy to EEG or fMRI findings is just absurd. Excitation of neurons is not the same thing as a thought--and neither is transparent from the readout of medical instruments. Unfortunately, such overstatement as this occurs throughout the essay.<br /><br />Surely Harris misspoke in the quotation here. But the overreaching it suggests is part and parcel of his approach. He is convinced, based on scant evidence, that prior events in the brain make our decisions for us. We may believe we decide but absolutely we don't. Indeed, the pioneering research of the late neurophysiologist Benjamin Libet and neuroscientist John-Dylan Haynes is provocative but it does not definitively obliterate free will.<br /><br />Philosopher Eddy Nahmias , writing in the Opinionator blog of The New York Times, has a more thoughtful and scientifically respectable approach to the research on free will. He says, \\"(T)he existing evidence does not support the conclusion that free will is an illusion. First of all, it does not show that a decision has been made before people are aware of having made it. It simply finds discernible patterns of neural activity that precede decisions. If we assumethat conscious decisions have neural correlates, then we should expect to find early signs of those correlates `ramping up' to the moment of consciousness. It would be miraculous if the brain did nothing at all until the moment people became aware of a decision....\\"<br /><br />Harris, though, in his over-eager way, wants certainty NOW. He's categorical:  Our choices appear in our minds as though \\"sprung from the void,\\" he states. Although neuroscience is in its infancy, he speaks as if this is the last word on the subject. Yet at this early stage we don't even know what consciousness is. And while the neural correlates are necessary, no one has yet proven that they are sufficient to cause conscious states. Someone with faith in another system might argue that we participate in consciousness rather than generating it on the spot. It is still the wild West out there in realm of neuroscience.<br /><br />Here's where Harris gets a gold star, however. He doesn't dodge the implications of his position. And this means that if people, even criminals, aren't ultimately responsible for what they do, our justice systems must take this into consideration. Not that dangerous people should be allowed to remain in society, but we should do all we can to rehabilitate them. Furthermore, we should eliminate all vestiges of vengeance from punishment meted out. Now that is taking a significant stand.<br /><br />Where Harris gets a failing grade is in philosophy. Yes, he considers determinism, libertarianism, and compatabilism. That's not the point. He neglects to acknowledge that  at present we have extremely limited data and extremely limited knowledge about what actually constitutes consciousness. Neuroscience is still in the cradle. It's way too soon to assume an end point has been reached and the ancient problem of free will resolved.<br /><br />Science is not static. Does Harris really believe that his position will hold steady after more and more competing labs have reported in?Does he really believe that further research will not subsume the research he is citing? Of course, no one knows what the future will bring. But if the history of science is any model, we are in the beginning stages of an ongoing dialectic.

0307888908|5.0|The basic premise in Reid Hoffman and Ben Casnocha's book is that the same mind-sets and skill-sets that can help to ensure the success of a start-up company's performance can also (with appropriate modification) help to ensure the success of an individual's career. In fact, all companies should always be viewed - and managed - as a start-up. This what Jack Welch had in mind years ago when, during a GE annual meeting, he explained why he admired entrepreneurial companies:<br /><br />\\"For one, they communicate better. Without the din and prattle of bureaucracy, people listen as well as talk; and since there are fewer of them they generally know and understand each other. Second, small companies move faster. They know the penalties for hesitation in the marketplace. Third, in small companies, with fewer layers and less camouflage, the leaders show up very clearly on the screen. Their performance and its impact are clear to everyone. And, finally, smaller companies waste less. They spend less time in endless reviews and approvals and politics and paper drills. They have fewer people; therefore they can only do the important things. Their people are free to direct their energy and attention toward the marketplace rather than fighting bureaucracy.\\"<br /><br />Hoffman and Casnocha assert, \\"To succeed professionally in today's world, you need to adopt entrepreneurial strategies...In the same way, you need to stay young and agile; you need to forever be a [begin italics] start-up [end italics].\\" Speaking for both of them (as he does throughout the book), Hoffman adds, \\"The business strategies employed by highly successful [begin italics] start-ups [end italics] and the career strategies employed by highly successful [begin italics] individuals [end italics] are strikingly similar.\\" Readers are introduced to several \\"strategic frameworks\\" within which valuable (usually counterintuitive) insights are revealed by exemplary entrepreneurs such as Hoffman and Casnocha  (of course) as well as Marc Andreesen, Jeff Bezos, Benjamin Franklin, Reed Hastings, Steve Jobs, Mary Sue Milliken, Marc Pinkus, Joseph Priestley, and Sheryl Sandberg, with insights anchored in their real-world experience.<br /><br />Although Hoffman and Casnocha carefully identify the \\"what\\" of what organizational and individual success requires, they focus most of their attention on how (and how not to) achieve it. 0807014273|5.0|Viktor Frankl's Man's Search for Meaning is probably one of the most impacting books I have ever read. The first part of his story, about his times in the concentration camps, manages to wrench the heart while still steering away from actively striving to highlight the suffering Frankl witnessed daily. Rather, his matter-of-fact tone and reserved yet descriptive telling of his stories in the concentration camps only serve to heighten the sense the reader gets of the trials he endured. But the most poignant part of the novel wasn't, for me, his stories but rather his overall message: that our greatest trait, and our greatest means of freedom, is the freedom to control our attitude towards any situation, no matter how horrendous (and here is where his experience in the concentration camps really serve to emphasize his point as well as his suffering and the suffering of others).<br /> The second part of the book, logotherapy, appealed to me almost more than the first portion did. Logotherapy focuses primarily on the goal of finding meaning in one's life. Upon reading the definition I thought, skeptic that I am, that sure--this is a great goal, but perchance all hot air. It wasn't until I read the rest of Frankl's portion on logotherapy, read as he related it to problems across all fields, from dissatisfaction at work to a loss of faith in one's own capabilities, and it wasn't until I went back and related these techniques that I realized how revolutionary Frankl's theories are.<br /> Of course logotherapy doesn't work for everyone and of course everyone will not agree with it, but believer in logotherapy or psychology or not, it would be a grievous error not to peruse Viktor Frankl's Man's Search for Meaning. It is a book that will change the way you look at the world, whether you take the psychology into consideration or not.

For example, you cannot aim your ball to angle across the lane. Rather you can roll a straight ball (no spin) or a hook (varied spin) which will hit the pins directly down the lane from where you roll the ball from. So if you roll from the middle, your ball will hit in the middle, with the amount of spin, or no spin, that you release it with.<br /><br />The real limitations of the game are that 1) there is no natural way to aim the ball, it only goes toward the point exactly opposite from where it is dropped, 2) you cannot pull up your scores from previous games, or the current scoreboard for that matter (it only shows between frames), 3) you cannot play against other human players or online opponents, and 4) as mentioned by another reviewer, you cannot track averages or have things like tournaments and special events.<br /><br />It is a nicely done app for what it is, but could use some good user experience QA to make it more real instead of just a time consumer. B002G1YPH0|5.0|I bought this cooler at Best Buy which is re-branded under it's Rocketfish label for a few bucks more. Other than the sticker on the fan, it's identical to the Coolermaster Hyper 212.<br /><br />I had problems keeping my 2.66GHz Core2 Quad Q9450 cool with the stock fan for years. I even got brand new high speed case fans. Even after that it was still idling at 70C. I'm surprised it didn't completely burn up with temps like that.<br /><br />I installed this guy, which was quite easy for ATX socket 775 motherboards. I removed the motherboard, but you should always do this when installing the cooler so you can be sure to get a snug fit, and make sure all the pins are secure. When dealing with something as sensitive as a CPU, you always need to be sure. I used Arctic Silver 5, since I had some lying around. It does come with a tube of compound, which I'm sure works at the very least nearly as good. It was large, but for a full size case there's breathing room. It may be problematic for some MicroATX cases and I'm sure it won't fit in MiniITX cases with half-height expansion slots. Also, if your RAM is close to the CPU socket, it may not fit, or you may need to move the closest RAM stick.<br /><br />The construction seemed solid, and it's even got copper heat pipes, which you typically see in more expensive products. In fact, this thing cooled just as good as a $50 solid copper Zalman I had years ago, which kept my old Sledgehammer Opteron at 45C. A C2Q and s940 Opteron both have around the same average operating temps, so to me it's a somewhat valid point of reference.<br /><br />The results speak for themselves, my CPU now idles at around 46C, which doesn't sound amazing since it's not overclocked, but when you consider the fact that it's a 23C improvement from the stock fan, what more can you ask for?<br /><br />The price is well worth it. Sure, the stock worked fine for 4 years even though it sucked, but I've got peace of mind in knowing that my computer won't go upin smoke seeing as it's so old.<br /><br />At it's current price ($27) I would recommend it over a free stock fan, obviously. I would also recommend it over any other aftermarket cooler, unless you find a great deal on a high-end enthusiast cooler.<br /><br />The only time I would stay away from this fan is when the memory banks are no more than an inch from the CPU socket, or smaller cases, unless you feel like rolling the dice.

0061122416|5.0|The novel The Alchemist, written by Paulo Coelho, is an intriguing fable about a boy, Santiago, on his quest to follow his dream. It is an inspiring book for people of all ages. Paulo Coelho, just like the Santiago in the story, followed his dream of becoming an author until his dream was reached. Growing up in Rio de Janeiro, Brazil, Paulo was on a journey following his dream through many different professions in his life; leading up to his profession in writing. He now has reached his dream after writing The Alchemist and continues to write in Rio de Janeiro today.<br /><br />  The Alchemist is a novel that anyone can relate to. This novel doesn't favor any gender or age group, so anyone can read it making it a great novel. The story is based off of Santiago's quest of finding his dream and the experiences learned throughout the trip. In the book Santiago states, \\"There is only one thing that makes a dream impossible to achieve: the fear of failure\\" (p 141). What Santiago says is very true, it is the fantasy of a dream that helps motivate people through life. Everyone has once had a dream and can relate to the obstacles that one is faced with while trying to pursue that dream. The big part of the novel is how Santiago decided to pursue his dream and didn't let anything or anyone get in the way of it; something many people try to do. This novel is for everyone and really helps one realize the importance of having a dream in their life.<br /><br />  Another aspect of the novel that really makes it interesting to read is Paulo's use of symbolism. Paulo really makes the reader think about the importance of each object and situation that Santiago encounters. A great example of symbolism is the desert that Santiago has to go through in order to reach the pyramids. The desert is full of danger and death because of the tribal wars and tough conditions. This symbolizes the obstacles and road blocks that someone goes through while pursuing their dream, or personal legion. If Santiago is able to get through the desert he will be very close to reaching the pyramids. The Alchemist explains the desert to Santiago, \\"The desert will give you an understanding of the world; in fact, anything on the face of the earth will do that.\\" This is just one of the many lessons that Santiago learned throughout his journey in the deserts. His travel through the desert taught him a lot of knowledge and patience in order to reach the pyramids. The symbol of the desert is just one of many examples of symbolism that makes the reader think.<br /><br />  This fable is something that everyone should read and will definitely enlighten your life. Paulo wrote this book so anyone can relate to the story and also makes the reader really think about what they have read. I definitely recommend this book and hope that you find it as inspiring as I have.

I listened to it straight through three times the first 48 hours I had it, and then listened to each song a few more times out of sequence.  It's solid, heavy, and suprisingly good.  I won't bore you with a breakdown of each track, but I will say that it does exactly what I want it to do....it rocks!  I play it in the garage while I'm tinkering around.  I play it at work while I'm cubicle-dwelling.  I play it in the car while I'm cruising.  I play it at the end of the day while I tip a few back.<br /><br />Sure, it has DLR's vocal range exposed and his sing-song talkie fluff at times.  Sure, it has some production issues (flatness, etc.).  Sure, I miss MA's backing vocals, but damn it, as a whole, it's well worth it.  If you like VH, buy it.  If you like rock, buy it.  I play it for my kids (5 & 9), so that they hear it next to Fair Warning and my other \\"oldies\\" and guess what, it holds up.  They like it, I like it.  It's not going to change the world, it's not the greatest ever, but hell, it fills a void in the current release apocalypse that we're stuck in.  I'll leave politics to others.  I'll leave personal grudges to others.  Instead, I'll keep this in my rotation and I'll stay frosty!

0307719219|5.0|Daron Acemoglu and James Robinson have teamed up to write a broad reaching work that combines political science, history, and economics in order to attempt to explain why nations fail. Rather than relying on tired, outdated, and offensive theories such as geography, culture, or outright ignorance of impoverished populations. The theory advanced in this book is far more simpler, and in my view much more plausible. The theory states that poor societies are poor because political power is narrowly concentrated in the hands of elites who are more concerned with their own power rather than society at large. Prosperous societies have widely distributed political and economic rights throughout levels of society rather than on a narrow elite.<br /><br />The authors than go about providing a plethora of evidence for their theory. Thus, the theory is well supported. However, the history tends to wander and shoot off in random directions at points that make it difficult to follow--in spite of the many engaging and occasionally humorous anecdotes. But it doesn't detract from the overall message of the book that exploitive political and economic institutions are a cause of poor societies. Blending information from different disciplines can be difficult, but I praise the authors for creating a readable, yet comprehensive book that is my favorite of 2012, so far.

B005LAIH2W|3.0|The writings of Edgar Rice Burroughs have greatly influenced sci-fi over the last 100 years. With John Carter the long road of taking the series of novels to the big screen has finally been realized. The result is a decidedly mixed bag and one that won't exactly hold direct appeal to any particular age or demographic.<br /><br />Based on the first novel \\"A Princess of Mars\\" the story centers on John Carter who finds himself transported to Barsoom (Mars) where he becomes involved in a war between factions on the planet. While he has little interest in taking sides he eventually forms allegiances while attempting to find his way back home.<br /><br />The film starts on Earth and really struggles there. The scenes are dull and the relevance of them are totally unclear. Once on Barsoom it begins to get more interesting though even then the sense of discovery isn't as compelling as would have been anticipated. Eventually the action picks up, the story gets its hooks in, and improvement comes through the second half of the movie which delivers a surprisingly strong conclusion considering the earlier struggles in getting the audience emotionally involved.<br /><br />While that sense of discovery is lacking there is feeling of wonder and imagination that shines through. Given that the source material is so old it doesn't always translate well but that forward-looking nature is easily identified. There are some decent laughs to be had but it's generally focused on providing adventure and it does a sufficient job of that.<br /><br />The special effects are suitable when keeping in mind that they're trying to convey scenes written 100 years ago and that means they won't always come across as realistic as one would imagine now. Outside of that though the alien creatures look great. The 3D is ultimately unnecessary - adding some depth to scenes but otherwise not enhancing the experience significantly.<br /><br />Despite some dreadful spots - which early in the film include jarring scene transitions - somehow I ended up enjoying the film as a whole. It's a tough one to recommend, and I suspect there will be opinions that stretch completely across the board, but there is a unique nature about it that struck me as being commendable. B003UN409Q|3.0|Calling this movie \\"The Doors\\" is misleading. It would be more accurate to call it \\"Jim Morrison and Those Other Guys In the Band,\\" since that is how it's presented.<br /><br />It's always tempting to latch onto a rock legend in these rock biopics, and Oliver Stone clings like a limpet to the ghost of Jim Morrison. Acid-soaked scenery and mysticism are in every scene, but Stone seems content to wallow in the rock'n'roll debauchery rather than get into Morrison's head.<br /><br />It opens with a voiceover of Jim Morrison's poetry, as we slowly fade into a stalled recording session. Then it flips back to 1949, as Morrison's family drives through the desert. The boy catches a glimpse of several Indians by the road -- and one of them dies as the family leaves.<br /><br />Then it flips ahead to Morrison's (Val Kilmer) years at college -- he crashes a party for a pretty girl, makes arty films, acid-trips, and devotes himself to poetry. Then his pal Ray Manzarek (Kyle McLachlan) creates a rock band, with Morrison's poetry and voice as the centerpiece. Soon The Doors become a fixture in L.A. -- and then a famed band.<br /><br />But as the Doors become more famous, Morrison increasingly loses himself in the messianic-Dionysian-rocker role that has been set out for him. He weds a witch-journalist (Katherine Quinlan) but loves his fey girlfriend Pamela (Meg Ryan). And when he outrages the authorities with the threat of public exposure, the spiral starts that will only lead to death.<br /><br />Stone certainly knew how to evoke the golden ages of rock'n'roll. Lots of sex, kinetic concerts, and bizarre behavior where Morrison jumps up on platforms and screams, \\"I am the Lizard King! I can do anything!\\" The whole movie just kind of sweeps you off your feet.<br /><br />And Stone is quite brilliant with the direction. The movie is filmed like an acid trip -- shifting multicoloured skies, Indian ghosts, shadows on the sand, and eerie lighting. And it touches on other aspects of the late sixties -- the Factory, Nico, and the belief that something violent and beautiful was about to erupt.<br /><br />Unfortunately, Stone seems so enamored of the atmosphere that he never quite gets around to making a biopic. The other Doors are relegated to a frame for Morrison's bizarre behavior, and after the first half hour, they basically fade away. And he gets too enamored of his own style with the Indian ghost, and the arty \\"dead man\\" final scenes.<br /><br />Val Kilmer does a shockingly good job with the languid, unpredictable energy of Morrison. He sings well too. Sadly, his performance is stuck in a one-note character -- Stone opts to potray him just as a random, sleazy, wild jerk, and we don't get a single insight into his thoughts. Sure, Morrison could be horrible. But this is Jim Morrison as rock cliche, not as a human being.<br /><br />And sadly, none of the other actors get much chance to expand on their roles -- the other Doors actors, including MacLachlan, basically play slightly annoyed accessories, and Meg Ryan plays a sweet girl without many other dimensions. Although Crispin Glover has a tiny, memorable role as Andy Warhol.<br /><br />\\"The Doors\\" opens into a beautiful psychedelic movie, but Stone fails to give it a soul. It's a fun biopic full of the sixties atmosphere, but it never quite manages to be much more.late sixties -- the Factory, Nico, and the belief that something violent and beautiful was about to erupt.<br /><br />Unfortunately, Stone seems so enamored of the atmosphere that he never quite gets around to making a biopic. B002MQYOFW|5.0|[This is kind of spoiler-ish]  I was reluctant to read this book, because I can't seem to get back into YA fiction, but, if the YA fiction of today means simple, concise, packed prose like this, that is so easily accessible, then I really need to read more of it.<br /><br />There is a great balance of exposition here, and just enough revealed, naturally or narratively.  I had an immediate connection to Katniss, which is something that usually takes a while to get into.  The story about her sister's cat was a great example of this, demonstrating both her toughness and the powerful love for her sister.  The first chapter is so powerful, it's a shame that the movie trailer gives the plot away.  The opening is also great, with the concept of bartering for tesserae.  While world-building can be stale, and not so creative, this brings out socioeconomic details, familial dynamic, and additionally, adds to the tension of the lottery.<br /><br />I don't know the terminology for this, but I enjoyed her use of experiential metaphor(?).  Using past experiences or thoughts as metaphor, instead of some sort of awkward correlation, helps us relate to the characters without being distracted by writing style.  Also, the use of memory along with the introduction of Peeta gives us perspective on how Katniss has gotten to where she is, and shows us her humanity.  If Katniss would have been the only person we would have gotten to know, we could have still really enjoyed the book, but only remained an observer.  But, when Peeta's true nature is revealed to Katniss on the train, our hearts quicken when we learn about the rules of the Hunger Games.  No child should have to grow up so soon.<br /><br />I also loved Haymitch's strategy.  It introduced a sort of device found in screwball comedies.  She is supposed to pretend to like someone she shouldn't like, and she is actually starting to like, but will never show she truly likes, except when she has to show she is pretending to.  Seems complicated, but adds so much the complexity of the relationships.<br /><br />I recalled stories of WWI, where, during Christmas time, everyone laid down arms, and celebrated the holiday, and, the next day, went back to trying to kill each other.  I was also reminded of how I was, at her age, feeling so strongly about something, and being in denial of what was really going on.  The passion of that age, that, in some respects, never comes again.<br />P.S.  I am such a sap and a sucker, and couldn't believe how emotional I was getting. I was emotionally manipulated as exactly as intended.  [shakes fist at Collins].  And, I am a HUGE fan of Jennifer Lawrence since I saw her in WINTER'S BONE, that I can't wait to see her in this movie.  So glad I read the book first, and you should do the same.

B00005JO5L|2.0|While I disagree with the Turkey (1-star) rating given by [[ASIN:0345449916 Mick Martin]]--at nine words, it was also one of the shortest reviews in the guide: \\"A horror in the real sense of the word.\\"--William Friedkin's 1980 film is very far from perfect.<br /><br />I have seen Cruising three times, each viewing separated by several months. The film is disturbing every time. It's not just the unbelievable level of risk (and nonexistent backup) that the undercover assignment affords. It's also the unease with which Friedkin retells the [[ASIN:0812813235 story]]. This seems to be a case of not knowing how offensive to be. Protesters from New York City's gay community besieged the on-location production, and Friedkin admits during the commentary track that post-production looping was necessary due to the noise. Richard Gere ([[ASIN:B00005AQMJ And the Band Played On]]) was said to have been Friedkin's first choice for the lead, and maybe the first instinct was right. Al Pacino (looking eerily like a young John Turturro) seems willing to get his hands dirty--some of the club scenes, which may or may not include stolen footage, evidence fisting--but he remains hamstrung by the script. Ultimately, the filmmakers got it wrong: the film is too disturbing for the general public and yet not disturbing enough for enthusiasts of the genre.<br /><br />More objectively, the film is simply not shot well. Or maybe the editing is to blame. A late scene of Pacino stalking his suspect goes dark except for a spotlight around Pacino. Apparently this is a \\"Director's Cut\\" tweak to make the scene's intent more clear. The same suspect's father, seen in flashback sequences, is a confusing and unsatisfying red herring. Moreover, the director's decision to interchange actors as the killer in an attempt to deliberately confuse the whodunit seems misguided. Cruising's ambiguity leaves this viewer unclear whether that was the goal or whether Friedkin simply could not decide what story to tell (or how to tell it). Too, the film suffers from an inability to connect emotionally with its hero.<br /><br />The Village Voice's Nathan Lee opined recently that Cruising is \\"a lurid fever dream of popper fumes, color-coded pocket hankies, hardcore disco frottage, and Crisco-coated forearms.\\" Certainly it remains a time capsule despite its many faults. Clubs like the Ramrod are long since gone and sadly so must be many of the men who played extras here. Unfortunately, this exploitative film does nothing to dispel the posited association between homosexuality and antisocial behavior. Witness Friedkin's decision to intersplice frames of hardcore gay pornography into some of the murder scenes.<br /><br />Somewhat infamously, the film and its director were nominated for the first ever RAZZIE Awards. Poor commentary track here in the Deluxe Edition: Director Friedkin alternates between simply describing the action and dead air. It is much less informative and engaging than his insight on [[ASIN:B0006GANN2 The French Connection]]. Better is the long interview with Friedkin from the September 2007 issue of Venice Magazine. B00664AM5C|3.0|The film \\"Contagion\\" plays on our media-fed fears of what might transpire during a global pandemic. The film traces the development of a mutant flu, its transmission, the response from the scientific and medical community, and possible societal consequences.<br /><br />The notion of zoonotic transmission of mutant flu strain lingers lack a nagging nightmare, and global interconnectedness and rapid transit via jets (think of them as disease incubators) across sets up the perfect storm. Couple that with the almost nonexistent screening for disease at airports, docks, and border crossings, and it is clear that an infectious disease outbreak in one part of the world can rapidly spread pretty much anywhere. \\"Contagion\\" taps into that fear and as the film shows in some of its strongest and well-crafted sections, in just a matter of days and weeks, a global pandemic could occur.<br /><br />Its initial depiction of the public health response to such an event seem realistic as epidemiologists plot the rate and route of transmission and infection, field staff try to cope with organizing a response as the health system is soon overwhelmed, and researchers beak out their microscopes and centrifuges.<br /><br />But this section is also the weakest part of the film. Public health researchers would actually be swarming the labs and facilities, not working in lonely pairs. The CDC director would not be calling his from a dark, empty room like the film's staged setting. The amount of activity would make a beehive in midsummer seem relaxed, and the film flubs this portrayal.<br /><br />The social and economic fabric would likely ravel to some degree, possibly as shown in the film. Desperate people would hoard food and drugs, other desperate people would loot, kill, and kidnap, and the healthy would in large measure shun the sick. Misinformation would proliferate via the Internet (e.g., the forsythia cure), and schools and workplaces would shut down. (Did anyone else keep waiting for the power to go off and wonder what Matt Damon and his daughter were finding to eat?)<br /><br />In real life, military-enforced quarantines and curfews might help curtail this breakdown---or perhaps fuel it---in some areas.<br /><br />I understand that movies cannot operate at the same pace as real-life, so the lack of temporal realism did not bother me that much---unlike the often pounding, jarring soundtrack that required keeping my remote available at all times.<br /><br />\\"Contagion\\" falls squarely in the middle of the pack, interesting enough, packed full of potential, but never quite good enough to trigger my \\"willing suspension of disbelief.\\"the power to go off and wonder what Matt Damon and his daughter were finding to eat?)<br /><br />In real life, military-enforced quarantines and curfews might help curtail this breakdown---or perhaps fuel it---in some areas.<br /><br />I understand that movies cannot operate at the same pace as real-life, so the lack of temporal realism did not bother me that much---unlike the often pounding, jarring soundtrack that required keeping my remote available at all times.<br /><br />\\"Contagion\\" falls squarely in the middle of the pack, interesting enough, packed full of potential, but never quite good enough to trigger my \\"willing suspension of disbelief.\\"

B0012GMW34|5.0|If you're a fan of John McLaughlin's music in general and especially of his jazz-fusion Mahavishnu Orchestra period from the 1970s, then you're going to love this album. If you're a Santana fan and don't know Carlos's music from this period so well, then this might be more of a surprise.<br /><br />The differing styles of these two great guitarists meld surprisingly well, as they knew each other and played together a lot. LDS is 100% instrumental (there's a background repetitive choral chant on one track, but this is not really a `song'). It's in the same vein as some of the lengthier and more complex pieces released on Santana's 1973 `Welcome' - on which McLaughlin guested - and `Borboletta' albums, and is particularly redolent of the sound on MO's `Lost Trident Sessions.'<br /><br />Both these musicians were during this period seeking after a higher spiritual meaning in life and devoted to the works of Sri Chinmoy (Santana later described Chinmoy as \\"vindictive\\" but said his association with the Indian had been a \\"good learning experience\\"). A spiritual intensity informs the music and it can't be described as `easy listening', but for those who appreciate the very best jazz-fusion played at a high level with virtuosity and passion, this is as good as it gets.<br /><br />Fellow members of the contemporary Santana band Michael Shrieve, Doug Rauch and Armando Peraza, and McLaughlin's fellow MO jazzmen drummer Billy Cobham and pianist Jan Hammer - described as playing `drums' in the sleeve notes - form fine backing combos on the various tracks and make the album a kind of summit-meeting for the jazz fusion innovators of the early 1970s.<br /><br />The original album came in at less than 30 minutes, but with no padding and every track a gem. The standout piece for most fans is the superlative `Let us go into the House of the Lord' with a captivating intensity which will likely leave you breathless. On this release, alternate takes of Trane's `A Love Supreme' with its insistent driving bass motif an anchor to the soaring guitar virtuosity, and `Naima' with its acoustic `Lotus-on-Irish-Streams' feel are offered as bonus tracks.nsistent driving bass motif an anchor to the soaring guitar virtuosity, and `Naima' with its acoustic `Lotus-on-Irish-Streams' feel are offered as bonus tracks.

0399159029|5.0|Bernie Gunther is a Nazi hating Berlin detective with a stellar reputation for excellence in solving crimes. Although this is a work of fiction, it involves actual historical facts as well as Nazi leaders. \\"Prague Fatale\\" takes place after the Nazis seized Czechoslovakia & Bohemia. Heydrich, the commander of the area, appointed by Hitler, sets up a lavish life-style in the seized Prague area chateau of Ferdinand Bloch-Bauer, who was a wealthy Jewish industrialist, his wife, Adele Bloch-Bauer, is the subject of the famous 1912 painting by Gustav Klimt.  The Bloch-Bauer's chief butler is retained by Heydrich to run the chateau. Gunther is in the midst of solving a murder of a Dutch worker made to appear as an accident on the rails in Berlin when he meets a woman under mysterious circumstances who may or may not be an escort. They form an amorous relationship. Heydrich, who knows Gunther is not a Nazi although he had been sent to the Eastern front to participate in atrocities, summons Gunther to the chateau along with a cadre of high-ranking Nazi officials for a weekend gathering. The guests are plied with food and drink; Heydrich explains that it is Gunther's job to protect him. During the weekend, a young officer is discovered dead in his room, locked from the inside. Heydrich gives Gunther carte blanche to interview everyone in the Chateau, from staff to Heydrich himself. Phillip Kerr's story unravels and references are made to Hercule Poirot, Agatha Christie's legendary fictional detective.<br /><br />This is an absorbing and interesting work set within the Nazi milieu. I highly recommend this to those who enjoy historically accurate settings for works of fiction. At the conclusion of the novel, Kerr's \\"author's Notes\\" provides information concerning the fates of all the real-life participants and the fate of the doomed Check resistance leaders.

B0001EFTVA|4.0|The most accessible film of director Satoshi Kon's all-too-brief anime career. Where Perfect Blue and Paprika are more adventurous and challenging, they're also difficult to sit back and enjoy in a traditional sense without falling into a deep state of analysis. This one, of three bickering homeless chums who find an infant girl in the garbage, retains the quirks, charms and emotional punch of Kon's other works without challenging quite so many conventions. Sweet, funny and grounded, it's constantly flashing a dry wit and, although it often tugs at the heart strings, things never get overly soft or weepy. The trio of leads are diverse and interesting, each with an onion skin of personal history to explore, and their hunt for the child's parents amidst the overpopulation of a major world metropolis constantly jolts out in surprising new directions. Beautifully written, drawn and animated, it can also be a bit static and randomly fortuitous.

B0052WKZZ6|5.0|There's no denying that this music comes from another place and has the power to transport the mind and soul of the listener. Oldfield composes carefully and expressively and the patient listener, without musical prejudice, can find in his first four albums something quite extraordinary. Incantations is the pinnacle of this stage of Oldfield's career and is the work of a composer at a creative peak. There is none of the whimsy or pastoral musings that can be found in Tubular Bells, Hergest Ridge or Ommadawn, but instead we have a solid exploration of a theme, a cycle of four separate pieces that hold together impressively.
